Title: Climate change

URL Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change

Published Time: 2001-10-30T17:25:26Z

Markdown Content:
This article is about the present-day human-induced rise in global temperatures. For natural historical climate trends, see [Climate variability and change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change "Climate variability and change").

[![Image 1: The global map shows sea temperature rises of 0.5 to 1 degree Celsius; land temperature rises of 1 to 2 degrees Celsius; and Arctic temperature rises of up to 4 degrees Celsius.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg/330px-Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg)

[![Image 2: Timeseries of global warming from 1880 to 2020 compared to simulated temperatures given only natural forcing. The first shows a positive trend since around 1950 and the second stays relatively flat.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg/330px-Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg)

Earth's average surface air temperature has increased almost 1.5°C (about 2.5°F) since the [Industrial Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_Revolution "Industrial Revolution"). Natural forces cause some variability, but the 20-year average shows the progressive influence of human activity.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-2)

Present-day **climate change** includes both **global warming**—the ongoing increase in [global average temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_surface_temperature "Global surface temperature")—and its wider effects on Earth's [climate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_system "Climate system"). [Climate change in a broader sense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change "Climate variability and change") also includes previous long-term changes to Earth's [climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate "Climate"). The current rise in global temperatures is [driven by human activities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_consensus_on_climate_change "Scientific consensus on climate change"), especially [fossil fuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel "Fossil fuel") burning since the [Industrial Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_Revolution "Industrial Revolution").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-3)[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Lynas_2021-4) Fossil fuel use, [deforestation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deforestation_and_climate_change "Deforestation and climate change"), and some [agricultural](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture "Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture") and industrial practices release [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Our_World_in_Data-2020-5) These gases [absorb some of the heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_effect "Greenhouse effect") that the Earth [radiates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_radiation "Thermal radiation") after it warms from sunlight, warming the lower atmosphere. [Carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide"), the primary gas driving global warming, [has increased in concentration by about 50%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere "Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere") since the [pre-industrial era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-industrial_society "Pre-industrial society") to levels not seen for millions of years.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-6)

Climate change has an increasingly large [impact on the environment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change "Effects of climate change"). [Deserts are expanding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desertification "Desertification"), while [heat waves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_wave "Heat wave") and [wildfires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfire#Climate_change_effects "Wildfire") are becoming more common.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-7)[Amplified warming in the Arctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_amplification "Polar amplification") has contributed to thawing [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost"), [retreat of glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850") and [sea ice decline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline "Arctic sea ice decline").[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-8) Higher temperatures are also causing [more intense storms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_and_climate_change "Tropical cyclones and climate change"), droughts, and other [weather extremes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_weather "Extreme weather").[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-9) Rapid environmental change in [mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montane_ecosystems "Montane ecosystems"), [coral reefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral_reef "Coral reef"), and [the Arctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic "Climate change in the Arctic") is forcing many species to relocate or [become extinct](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_climate_change "Extinction risk from climate change").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-10) Even if efforts to minimize future warming are successful, some effects will continue for centuries. These include [ocean heating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_temperature#Increasing_temperature_due_to_climate_change "Ocean temperature"), [ocean acidification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_acidification "Ocean acidification") and [sea level rise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level_rise "Sea level rise").[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-11)

Climate change [threatens people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_human_health "Effects of climate change on human health") with increased flooding, extreme heat, increased [food](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture "Effects of climate change on agriculture") and [water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_scarcity#Climate_change "Water scarcity") scarcity, more disease, and [economic loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_impacts_of_climate_change "Economic impacts of climate change").[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-12)[Human migration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_migrant "Environmental migrant") and conflict can also be a result.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-13) The [World Health Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Organization "World Health Organization") calls climate change one of the biggest threats to [global health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_health "Global health") in the 21st century.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-WHO_Nov_2023-14) Societies and ecosystems will experience more severe risks without [action to limit warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation "Climate change mitigation").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-15)[Adapting to climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation "Climate change adaptation") through efforts like [flood control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flood_control "Flood control") measures or [drought-resistant crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xerophyte "Xerophyte") partially reduces climate change risks, although some limits to adaptation have already been reached.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-16) Poorer communities are responsible for [a small share of global emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_justice "Climate justice"), yet have the least ability to adapt and are most [vulnerable to climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_vulnerability "Climate change vulnerability").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-17)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-18)

[![Image 3: Bobcat Fire in Monrovia, CA, September 10, 2020](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Bobcat_Fire%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_San_Gabriel_Mountains.jpg/330px-Bobcat_Fire%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_San_Gabriel_Mountains.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bobcat_Fire,_Los_Angeles,_San_Gabriel_Mountains.jpg)

[![Image 4: Bleached colony of Acropora coral](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bleached_colony_of_Acropora_coral.jpg/250px-Bleached_colony_of_Acropora_coral.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bleached_colony_of_Acropora_coral.jpg)

[![Image 5: A dry lakebed in California, which is experiencing its worst megadrought in 1,200 years.[19]](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/California_Drought_Dry_Lakebed_2009.jpg/250px-California_Drought_Dry_Lakebed_2009.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:California_Drought_Dry_Lakebed_2009.jpg)

Many climate change impacts have been observed in the first decades of the 21st century, with 2024 the warmest on record at +1.60°C (2.88°F) since regular tracking began in 1850.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-20)[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-21) Additional warming will increase these impacts and can trigger [tipping points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tipping_points_in_the_climate_system "Tipping points in the climate system"), such as melting all of the [Greenland ice sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet "Greenland ice sheet").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-22) Under the 2015 [Paris Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris_Agreement "Paris Agreement"), nations collectively agreed to keep warming "well under 2°C". However, with pledges made under the Agreement, global warming would still reach about 2.8°C (5.0°F) by the end of the century.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-UNEP2024-23) Limiting warming to 1.5°C would require halving emissions by 2030 and achieving [net-zero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_neutrality "Carbon neutrality") emissions by 2050.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-24)[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-25)

There is widespread support for [climate action](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_action "Climate action") worldwide.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Damian-26)[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Peter-27)[Fossil fuels can be phased out](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out "Fossil fuel phase-out") by stopping [subsidising them](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel_subsidies "Fossil fuel subsidies"), [conserving energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conserving_energy "Conserving energy") and switching to [energy sources that do not produce significant carbon pollution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low-carbon_electricity "Low-carbon electricity"). These energy sources include wind, solar, hydro, and nuclear power.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-28) Cleanly generated electricity can replace fossil fuels for [powering transportation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_vehicles "Electric vehicles"), [heating buildings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_heating "Electric heating"), and running industrial processes.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-29) Carbon can also be [removed from the atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal "Carbon dioxide removal"), for instance by [increasing forest cover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest_protection "Forest protection") and farming with methods that [store carbon in soil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_farming "Carbon farming").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-30)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-31)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-32)

Terminology
-----------

Before the 1980s, it was unclear whether the warming effect of [increased greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_effect "Greenhouse effect") was stronger than the [cooling effect of airborne particulates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particulates#Climate_effects "Particulates") in air pollution. Scientists used the term _inadvertent climate modification_ to refer to human impacts on the climate at this time.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Conway_2008-33) In the 1980s, the terms _global warming_ and _climate change_ became more common, often being used interchangeably.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-34)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-35)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-36) Scientifically, _global warming_ refers only to increased global average surface temperature, while _climate change_ describes both global warming and its effects on Earth's [climate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_system "Climate system"), such as precipitation changes.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Conway_2008-33)

_Climate change_ can also be used more broadly to include [changes to the climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change "Climate variability and change") that have happened throughout Earth's history.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-37)_Global warming_—used as early as 1975[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Science_Broecker_19750808-38)—became the more popular term after [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") climate scientist [James Hansen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Hansen "James Hansen") used it in his 1988 testimony in the [U.S. Senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Senate "U.S. Senate").[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-history.aip.org2-39) Since the 2000s, _climate change_ has increased usage.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-40) Various scientists, politicians and media may use the terms _[climate crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate\_crisis "Climate crisis")_ or _[climate emergency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate\_emergency\_declaration "Climate emergency declaration")_ to talk about climate change, and may use the term _global heating_ instead of _global warming_.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-41)[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-42)

Global temperature rise
-----------------------

### Temperatures prior to present-day global warming

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Common_Era_Temperature.svg/500px-Common_Era_Temperature.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Common_Era_Temperature.svg)

[Global surface temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_surface_temperature "Global surface temperature") reconstruction over the past 2000 years using proxy data from tree rings, corals, and ice cores in blue.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-43) Directly observed data is in red.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-nasa_temperatures-44)

Over the last few million years the climate cycled through [ice ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_period "Glacial period"). One of the hotter periods was the [Last Interglacial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_Interglacial "Last Interglacial"), around 125,000 years ago, where temperatures were between 0.5°C and 1.5°C warmer than before the start of global warming.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR6_WG1_Ch22021294,_296-45) This period saw sea levels 5 to 10 metres higher than today. The most [recent glacial maximum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum "Last Glacial Maximum") 20,000 years ago was some 5–7°C colder. This period has sea levels that were over 125 metres (410 ft) lower than today.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR6_WG1_Ch22021366-46)

Temperatures stabilized in the current interglacial period beginning [11,700 years ago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holocene "Holocene").[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-47) This period also saw the start of agriculture.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR6_WG1_Ch22021296-48) Historical patterns of warming and cooling, like the [Medieval Warm Period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period "Medieval Warm Period") and the [Little Ice Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Little_Ice_Age "Little Ice Age"), did not occur at the same time across different regions. Temperatures may have reached as high as those of the late 20th century in a limited set of regions.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-49)[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-50) Climate information for that period comes from [climate proxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxy_(climate) "Proxy (climate)"), such as trees and [ice cores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_core "Ice core").[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-SR15_Ch1_p57-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-52)

### Warming since the Industrial Revolution

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/1951-_Percent_of_record_temperatures_that_are_cold_or_warm_records.svg/500px-1951-_Percent_of_record_temperatures_that_are_cold_or_warm_records.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1951-_Percent_of_record_temperatures_that_are_cold_or_warm_records.svg)

 In recent decades, new high temperature records have substantially outpaced new low temperature records on a growing portion of Earth's surface.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-NOAA_July-53)

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/1955-_Ocean_heat_content_-_NOAA.svg/500px-1955-_Ocean_heat_content_-_NOAA.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1955-_Ocean_heat_content_-_NOAA.svg)

There has been an increase in [ocean heat content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content") during recent decades as the oceans absorb over 90% of the [heat from global warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget "Earth's energy budget").[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-NOAA_NASA_OHC_1957_-54)

Around 1850 [thermometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermometer "Thermometer") records began to provide global coverage.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-AR5_WG1_SPM_p4-5-55) Between the 18th century and 1970 there was little net warming, as the warming impact of greenhouse gas emissions was offset by cooling from [sulfur dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide "Sulfur dioxide") emissions. Sulfur dioxide causes [acid rain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_rain "Acid rain"), but it also produces [sulfate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfate "Sulfate") aerosols in the atmosphere, which reflect sunlight and cause [global dimming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_dimming "Global dimming"). After 1970, the increasing accumulation of greenhouse gases and controls on sulfur pollution led to a marked increase in temperature.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-56)[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-NASA2007-57)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Quaas2022-58)

NASA animation portraying global surface temperature changes from 1880 to 2023. The colour blue denotes cooler temperatures and red denotes warmer temperatures.[_[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please\_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_]

Ongoing changes in climate have had no precedent for several thousand years.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-59) Multiple datasets all show worldwide increases in surface temperature,[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-60) at a rate of around 0.2°C per decade.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-61) The 2014–2023 decade warmed to an average 1.19°C [1.06–1.30°C] compared to the pre-industrial baseline (1850–1900).[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-62) Not every single year was warmer than the last: internal [climate variability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability "Climate variability") processes can make any year 0.2°C warmer or colder than the average.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Samset2020-63) From 1998 to 2013, negative phases of two such processes, [Pacific Decadal Oscillation (PDO)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_decadal_oscillation "Pacific decadal oscillation")[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-SeipGr%C3%B8nWang2023PacificDecadalOscillation-64) and [Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation (AMO)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_multidecadal_oscillation "Atlantic multidecadal oscillation")[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-65) caused a short slower period of warming called the "[global warming hiatus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming_hiatus "Global warming hiatus")".[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-66) After the "hiatus", the opposite occurred, with 2024 well above the recent average at more than +1.5°C.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-67) This is why the temperature change is defined in terms of a 20-year average, which reduces the noise of hot and cold years and decadal climate patterns, and detects the long-term signal.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-IPCC_AR6_WGI_SPM-68): 5 [[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-69)

A wide range of other observations reinforce the evidence of warming.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-70)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoeb_et_al.2021-71) The upper atmosphere is cooling, because [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas") are trapping heat near the Earth's surface, and so less heat is radiating into space.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-72) Warming reduces average snow cover and [forces the retreat of glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850"). At the same time, warming also causes [greater evaporation from the oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle "Effects of climate change on the water cycle"), leading to more [atmospheric humidity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specific_humidity "Specific humidity"), more and heavier [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation").[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-73)[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-74) Plants are [flowering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flowering "Flowering") earlier in spring, and thousands of animal species have been permanently moving to cooler areas.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-75)

#### Differences by region

Different regions of the world [warm at different rates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change#Variability_between_regions "Climate variability and change"). The pattern is independent of where greenhouse gases are emitted, because the gases persist long enough to diffuse across the planet. Since the pre-industrial period, the average surface temperature over land regions has increased almost twice as fast as the global average surface temperature.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-76) This is because oceans lose more heat by [evaporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evaporation "Evaporation") and [oceans can store a lot of heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content").[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-77) The thermal energy in the global climate system has grown with only brief pauses since at least 1970, and over 90% of this extra energy has been [stored in the ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_heat_content "Ocean heat content").[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-ocean_heat_absorption-78)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Harvipccar5-79) The rest has heated the [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere "Atmosphere"), melted ice, and warmed the continents.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-EarthSysSciData_20200907-80)

The [Northern Hemisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere "Northern Hemisphere") and the North Pole have warmed much faster than the South Pole and Southern Hemisphere. The Northern Hemisphere not only has much more land, but also more seasonal snow cover and [sea ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_ice "Sea ice"). As these surfaces flip from reflecting a lot of light to being dark after the ice has melted, they start [absorbing more heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo").[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-81) Local [black carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_carbon "Black carbon") deposits on snow and ice also contribute to Arctic warming.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-82) Arctic surface temperatures are increasing [between three and four times faster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_amplification "Polar amplification") than in the rest of the world.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-3X2021-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Rantanen2022-84)[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-4X2021-85) Melting of [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheet "Ice sheet") near the poles weakens both the [Atlantic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_meridional_overturning_circulation "Atlantic meridional overturning circulation") and the [Antarctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Ocean_overturning_circulation "Southern Ocean overturning circulation") limb of [thermohaline circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation "Thermohaline circulation"), which further changes the distribution of heat and [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation "Precipitation") around the globe.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-86)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-PearceYale3602023-87)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Lee2023-88)[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-NOAA2023-89)

### Future global temperatures

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Projected_Change_in_Temperatures.svg/500px-Projected_Change_in_Temperatures.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Projected_Change_in_Temperatures.svg)

[CMIP6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coupled_Model_Intercomparison_Project#CMIP_Phase_6 "Coupled Model Intercomparison Project") multi-model projections of [global surface temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_surface_temperature "Global surface temperature") changes for the year 2090 relative to the 1850–1900 average. The current trajectory for warming by the end of the century is roughly halfway between these two extremes.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-UNEP2024-23)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Schuur2022-90)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Phiddian2022-91)

The [World Meteorological Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization "World Meteorological Organization") estimates there is almost a 50% chance of the five-year average global temperature exceeding +1.5°C between 2024 and 2028.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWMO2024b2-92) The IPCC expects the 20-year average to exceed +1.5°C in the early 2030s.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-:1-93)

The [IPCC Sixth Assessment Report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report "IPCC Sixth Assessment Report") (2021) included projections that by 2100 global warming is very likely to reach 1.0–1.8°C under a [scenario with very low emissions of greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways#SSP1:_Sustainability_(Taking_the_Green_Road) "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"), 2.1–3.5°C under an [intermediate emissions scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways#SSP3:_Regional_rivalry_(A_Rocky_Road) "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways"), or 3.3–5.7°C under [a very high emissions scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways#SSP5:_Fossil-Fueled_Development_(Taking_the_Highway) "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways").[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-94) The warming will continue past 2100 in the intermediate and high emission scenarios,[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Meinshausen2011-95)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Lyon2021-96) with future projections of global surface temperatures by year 2300 being similar to millions of years ago.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-97)

The remaining [carbon budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_budget "Carbon budget") for staying beneath certain temperature increases is determined by modelling the carbon cycle and [climate sensitivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_sensitivity "Climate sensitivity") to greenhouse gases.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-98) According to [UNEP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNEP "UNEP"), global warming can be kept below 1.5°C with a 50% chance if emissions after 2023 do not exceed 200 gigatonnes of CO 2. This corresponds to around 4 years of current emissions. To stay under 2.0°C, the carbon budget is 900 gigatonnes of CO 2, or 16 years of current emissions.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations_Environment_Programme2024XI,_XVII-99)

Causes of recent global temperature rise
----------------------------------------

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Physical_Drivers_of_climate_change.svg/500px-Physical_Drivers_of_climate_change.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Physical_Drivers_of_climate_change.svg)

[Physical drivers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiative_forcing "Radiative forcing") of global warming that has happened so far. Future [global warming potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming_potential "Global warming potential") for long lived drivers like carbon dioxide emissions is not represented. Whiskers on each bar show the possible [error range](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observational_error "Observational error").

The climate system experiences various cycles on its own which can last for years, decades or even centuries. For example, [El Niño](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o "El Niño") events cause short-term spikes in surface temperature while [La Niña](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a "La Niña") events cause short term cooling.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-100) Their relative frequency can affect global temperature trends on a decadal timescale.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-101) Other changes are caused by an [imbalance of energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_Energy_Imbalance "Earth's Energy Imbalance") from [external forcings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_forcing "Climate forcing").[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-102) Examples of these include changes in the concentrations of [greenhouse gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas "Greenhouse gas"), [solar luminosity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_luminosity "Solar luminosity"), [volcanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcano "Volcano") eruptions, and [variations in the Earth's orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_forcing "Orbital forcing") around the Sun.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-103)

To determine the human contribution to climate change, unique "fingerprints" for all potential causes are developed and compared with both observed patterns and known internal [climate variability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_variability "Climate variability").[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-104) For example, solar forcing—whose fingerprint involves warming the entire atmosphere—is ruled out because only the lower atmosphere has warmed.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP-2009-105) Atmospheric aerosols produce a smaller, cooling effect. Other drivers, such as changes in [albedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo"), are less impactful.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-106)

### Greenhouse gases

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg/500px-Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg)

CO 2 concentrations over the last 800,000 years as measured from ice cores (blue/green) and directly (black)

Greenhouse gases are transparent to [sunlight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunlight "Sunlight"), and thus allow it to pass through the atmosphere to heat the Earth's surface. The Earth [radiates it as heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiative_cooling "Radiative cooling"), and greenhouse gases absorb a portion of it. This absorption slows the rate at which heat escapes into space, trapping heat near the Earth's surface and warming it over time.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-107)

While [water vapour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapour "Water vapour") (≈50%) and clouds (≈25%) are the biggest contributors to the greenhouse effect, they primarily change as a function of temperature and are therefore mostly considered to be [feedbacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_feedbacks "Climate change feedbacks") that change [climate sensitivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_sensitivity "Climate sensitivity"). On the other hand, concentrations of gases such as CO 2 (≈20%), [tropospheric ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone "Tropospheric ozone"),[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-108)[CFCs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon "Chlorofluorocarbon") and [nitrous oxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrous_oxide "Nitrous oxide") are added or removed independently from temperature, and are therefore considered to be [external forcings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiative_forcing "Radiative forcing") that change global temperatures.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-109)

Before the Industrial Revolution, naturally occurring amounts of greenhouse gases caused the air near the surface to be about 33°C warmer than it would have been in their absence.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-110)[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-111) Human activity since the Industrial Revolution, mainly extracting and burning fossil fuels (coal, [oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petroleum "Petroleum"), and natural gas),[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-112) has increased the amount of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere. In 2022, the [concentrations of CO 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere "Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere") and methane had increased by about 50% and 164%, respectively, since 1750.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-113) These CO 2 levels are higher than they have been at any time during the last 14 million years.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Cenozoic_CO2_Proxy_Integration_Project_(CenCOPIP)_Consortium2023-114)[Concentrations of methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_methane "Atmospheric methane") are far higher than they were over the last 800,000 years.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR6_WG1_Technical_Summary2021TS-35-115)

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/CO2_Emissions_by_Source_Since_1880.svg/500px-CO2_Emissions_by_Source_Since_1880.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:CO2_Emissions_by_Source_Since_1880.svg)

The [Global Carbon Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Carbon_Project "Global Carbon Project") shows how additions to CO 2 since 1880 have been caused by different sources ramping up one after another.

Global human-caused greenhouse gas emissions in 2019 were [equivalent to](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming_potential "Global warming potential") 59 billion tonnes of CO 2. Of these emissions, 75% was CO 2, 18% was [methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane"), 4% was nitrous oxide, and 2% was [fluorinated gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluorinated_gases "Fluorinated gases").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR6_WG3_Summary_for_Policymakers2022Figure_SPM.1-116) CO 2 emissions primarily come from burning [fossil fuels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_fuel "Fossil fuel") to provide energy for transport, manufacturing, [heating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heating#Energy_sources "Heating"), and electricity.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Our_World_in_Data-2020-5) Additional CO 2 emissions come from [deforestation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deforestation_and_climate_change "Deforestation and climate change") and [industrial processes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_processes#Chemical_processes_by_main_basic_material "Industrial processes"), which include the CO 2 released by the chemical reactions for [making cement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cement#Chemistry "Cement"), [steel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blast_furnace#Process_engineering_and_chemistry "Blast furnace"), [aluminium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process "Hall–Héroult process"), and [fertilizer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haber_process "Haber process").[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-117)[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-118)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-119)[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-120) Methane emissions [come from livestock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enteric_fermentation "Enteric fermentation"), manure, [rice cultivation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_rice_cultivation "Environmental impact of rice cultivation"), landfills, wastewater, and [coal mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coal_seam_gas "Coal seam gas"), as well as [oil and gas extraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fugitive_gas_emissions "Fugitive gas emissions").[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-121)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-122) Nitrous oxide emissions largely come from the microbial decomposition of [fertilizer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fertilizer "Fertilizer").[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-123)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-124)

While methane only lasts in the atmosphere for an average of 12 years,[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-125) CO 2 lasts much longer. The Earth's surface absorbs CO 2 as part of the [carbon cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_cycle "Carbon cycle"). While plants on land and in the ocean absorb most excess emissions of CO 2 every year, that CO 2 is returned to the atmosphere when biological matter is digested, burns, or decays.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-nasacc-126) Land-surface [carbon sink](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_sink "Carbon sink") processes, such as [carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation") in the soil and photosynthesis, remove about 29% of annual global CO 2 emissions.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-127) The ocean has absorbed 20 to 30% of emitted CO 2 over the last two decades.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-128) CO 2 is only removed from the atmosphere for the long term when it is stored in the Earth's crust, which is a process that can take millions of years to complete.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-nasacc-126)

### Land surface changes

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/20210331_Global_tree_cover_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg/500px-20210331_Global_tree_cover_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:20210331_Global_tree_cover_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg)

The rate of global tree cover loss has approximately doubled since 2001, to an annual loss approaching an area the size of Italy.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-WRI_20240424-129)

Around 30% of Earth's land area is largely unusable for humans ([glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacier "Glacier"), [deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert "Desert"), etc.), 26% is forests, 10% is [shrubland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrubland "Shrubland") and 34% is [agricultural land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agricultural_land "Agricultural land").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-130)[Deforestation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deforestation "Deforestation") is the main [land use change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_use_change "Land use change") contributor to global warming,[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-131) as the destroyed trees release CO 2, and are not replaced by new trees, removing that [carbon sink](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_sink "Carbon sink").[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-IPCC_SRCCL_Summary_for_Policymakers_2019_18-132) Between 2001 and 2018, 27% of deforestation was from permanent clearing to enable [agricultural expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agricultural_expansion "Agricultural expansion") for crops and livestock. Another 24% has been lost to temporary clearing under the [shifting cultivation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shifting_cultivation "Shifting cultivation") agricultural systems. 26% was due to logging for wood and derived products, and [wildfires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfire "Wildfire") have accounted for the remaining 23%.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-133) Some forests have not been fully cleared, but were already degraded by these impacts. Restoring these forests also recovers their potential as a carbon sink.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Duchelle-2022-134)

Local vegetation cover impacts how much of the sunlight gets reflected back into space ([albedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo")), and how much [heat is lost by evaporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evaporative_cooling "Evaporative cooling"). For instance, the change from a dark forest to grassland makes the surface lighter, causing it to reflect more sunlight. Deforestation can also modify the release of chemical compounds that influence clouds, and by changing wind patterns.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Seymour_2019-135) In tropic and temperate areas the net effect is to produce significant warming, and forest restoration can make local temperatures cooler.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Duchelle-2022-134) At latitudes closer to the poles, there is a cooling effect as forest is replaced by snow-covered (and more reflective) plains.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Seymour_2019-135) Globally, these increases in surface albedo have been the dominant direct influence on temperature from land use change. Thus, land use change to date is estimated to have a slight cooling effect.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-IPCC_Special_Report:_Climate_change_and_Land_p2-54-136)

### Other factors

#### Aerosols and clouds

Air pollution, in the form of [aerosols, affects the climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particulates#Climate_effects "Particulates") on a large scale.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-137) Aerosols scatter and absorb solar radiation. From 1961 to 1990, a gradual reduction in the amount of [sunlight reaching the Earth's surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irradiance "Irradiance") was observed. This phenomenon is popularly known as _[global dimming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global\_dimming "Global dimming")_,[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-138) and is primarily attributed to [sulfate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfate "Sulfate") aerosols produced by the combustion of fossil fuels with heavy sulfur concentrations like coal and [bunker fuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bunker_fuel "Bunker fuel").[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Quaas2022-58) Smaller contributions come from [black carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_carbon "Black carbon") (from combustion of fossil fuels and [biomass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass "Biomass")), and from dust.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-139)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-140)[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-141) Globally, aerosols have been declining since 1990 due to pollution controls, meaning that they no longer mask greenhouse gas warming as much.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-142)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Quaas2022-58)

Aerosols also have indirect effects on the [Earth's energy budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget "Earth's energy budget"). Sulfate aerosols act as [cloud condensation nuclei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei "Cloud condensation nuclei") and lead to clouds that have more and smaller cloud droplets. These clouds reflect solar radiation more efficiently than clouds with fewer and larger droplets.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-143) They also reduce the [growth of raindrops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_physics#Collision-coalescence "Cloud physics"), which makes clouds more reflective to incoming sunlight.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-144) Indirect effects of aerosols are the largest uncertainty in [radiative forcing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiative_forcing "Radiative forcing").[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP_2017_ch2-145)

While aerosols typically limit global warming by reflecting sunlight, [black carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_carbon "Black carbon") in [soot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soot "Soot") that falls on snow or ice can contribute to global warming. Not only does this increase the absorption of sunlight, it also increases melting and sea-level rise.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-146) Limiting new black carbon deposits in the Arctic could reduce global warming by 0.2°C by 2050.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-147) The effect of decreasing sulfur content of fuel oil for ships since 2020[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-148) is estimated to cause an additional 0.05°C increase in global mean temperature by 2050.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-149)

#### Solar and volcanic activity

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/2017_Global_warming_attribution_-_based_on_NCA4_Fig_3.3_-_single-panel_version.svg/500px-2017_Global_warming_attribution_-_based_on_NCA4_Fig_3.3_-_single-panel_version.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2017_Global_warming_attribution_-_based_on_NCA4_Fig_3.3_-_single-panel_version.svg)

 The [Fourth National Climate Assessment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourth_National_Climate_Assessment "Fourth National Climate Assessment") ("NCA4", USGCRP, 2017) includes charts illustrating that neither solar nor volcanic activity can explain the observed warming.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-150)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-151)

As the Sun is the Earth's primary energy source, changes in incoming sunlight directly affect the [climate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_system "Climate system").[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP_2017_ch2-145)[Solar irradiance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_variation "Solar variation") has been measured directly by [satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite "Satellite"),[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-152) and indirect measurements are available from the early 1600s onwards.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP_2017_ch2-145) Since 1880, there has been no upward trend in the amount of the Sun's energy reaching the Earth, in contrast to the warming of the lower atmosphere (the [troposphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troposphere "Troposphere")).[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-153) The upper atmosphere (the [stratosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratosphere "Stratosphere")) would also be warming if the Sun was sending more energy to Earth, but instead, it has been cooling.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP-2009-105) This is consistent with greenhouse gases preventing heat from leaving the Earth's atmosphere.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-154)

[Explosive volcanic eruptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions#Plinian "Types of volcanic eruptions") can release gases, dust and ash that partially block sunlight and reduce temperatures, or they can send water vapour into the atmosphere, which adds to greenhouse gases and increases temperatures.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-155) These impacts on temperature only last for several years, because both water vapour and volcanic material have low persistence in the atmosphere.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP_Chapter_2_2017_79-156)[volcanic CO 2 emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanic_gas "Volcanic gas") are more persistent, but they are equivalent to less than 1% of current human-caused CO 2 emissions.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischerAiuppa2020-157) Volcanic activity still represents the single largest natural impact (forcing) on temperature in the industrial era. Yet, like the other natural forcings, it has had negligible impacts on global temperature trends since the Industrial Revolution.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP_Chapter_2_2017_79-156)

#### Climate change feedbacks

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/NORTH_POLE_Ice_%2819626661335%29.jpg/500px-NORTH_POLE_Ice_%2819626661335%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NORTH_POLE_Ice_(19626661335).jpg)

Sea ice reflects 50% to 70% of incoming sunlight, while the ocean, being darker, reflects only 6%. As an area of sea ice melts and exposes more ocean, more heat is absorbed by the ocean, raising temperatures that melt still more ice. This is a positive feedback [process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice%E2%80%93albedo_feedback "Ice–albedo feedback").[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-158)

The climate system's response to an initial forcing is shaped by feedbacks, which either amplify or dampen the change. _[Self-reinforcing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive\_feedback "Positive feedback")_ or _positive_ feedbacks increase the response, while _[balancing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative\_feedback "Negative feedback")_ or _negative_ feedbacks reduce it.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-159) The main reinforcing feedbacks are the [water-vapour feedback](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapour_feedback "Water vapour feedback"), the [ice–albedo feedback](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice%E2%80%93albedo_feedback "Ice–albedo feedback"), and the net [cloud feedback](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_feedback "Cloud feedback").[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUSGCRP_Chapter_2201789%E2%80%9391-160)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-161) The primary balancing mechanism is [radiative cooling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiative_cooling "Radiative cooling"), as Earth's surface gives off more [heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") to space in response to rising temperature.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUSGCRP_Chapter_2201789%E2%80%9390-162) In addition to temperature feedbacks, there are feedbacks in the carbon cycle, such as the fertilizing effect of CO 2 on plant growth.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-163) Feedbacks are expected to trend in a positive direction as greenhouse gas emissions continue, raising climate sensitivity.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-164)

These feedback processes alter the pace of global warming. For instance, warmer air [can hold more moisture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relative_humidity "Relative humidity") in the form of [water vapour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor "Water vapor"), which is itself a potent greenhouse gas.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUSGCRP_Chapter_2201789%E2%80%9391-160) Warmer air can also make clouds higher and thinner, and therefore more insulating, increasing climate warming.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsCeppiKatavouta2020-165) The reduction of snow cover and sea ice in the Arctic is another major feedback, this reduces the reflectivity of the Earth's surface in the region and [accelerates Arctic warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_amplification "Arctic amplification").[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-166)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-167) This additional warming also contributes to [permafrost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permafrost "Permafrost") thawing, which releases methane and CO 2 into the atmosphere.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Turetsky_2019-168)

Around half of human-caused CO 2 emissions have been absorbed by land plants and by the oceans.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-169) This fraction is not static and if future CO 2 emissions decrease, the Earth will be able to absorb up to around 70%. If they increase substantially, it'll still absorb more carbon than now, but the overall fraction will decrease to below 40%.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-170) This is because climate change increases droughts and heat waves that eventually inhibit plant growth on land, and soils will release more carbon from dead plants [when they are warmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_carbon_feedback "Soil carbon feedback").[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-171)[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-172) The rate at which oceans absorb atmospheric carbon will be lowered as they become more acidic and experience changes in [thermohaline circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation "Thermohaline circulation") and [phytoplankton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phytoplankton "Phytoplankton") distribution.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUSGCRP_Chapter_2201793%E2%80%9395-173)[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Liu2022-174)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-PearceYale3602023-87) Uncertainty over feedbacks, particularly cloud cover,[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-175) is the major reason why different climate models project different magnitudes of warming for a given amount of emissions.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-176)

Modelling
---------

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Greenhouse_Effect_%282017_NASA_data%29.svg/500px-Greenhouse_Effect_%282017_NASA_data%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect_(2017_NASA_data).svg)

Energy flows between space, the atmosphere, and Earth's surface. Most sunlight passes through the atmosphere to heat the Earth's surface, then greenhouse gases absorb most of the heat the Earth radiates in response. Adding to greenhouse gases increases this insulating effect, causing an [energy imbalance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget "Earth's energy budget") that heats the planet up.

A [climate model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_model "Climate model") is a representation of the physical, chemical and biological processes that affect the climate system.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-177) Models include natural processes like changes in the Earth's orbit, historical changes in the Sun's activity, and volcanic forcing.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-178) Models are used to estimate the degree of warming future emissions will cause when accounting for the [strength of climate feedbacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_sensitivity "Climate sensitivity").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-179)[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-180) Models also predict the circulation of the oceans, the annual cycle of the seasons, and the flows of carbon between the land surface and the atmosphere.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-181)

The physical realism of models is tested by examining their ability to simulate current or past climates.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-182) Past models have underestimated the rate of [Arctic shrinkage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_shrinkage "Arctic shrinkage")[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-183) and underestimated the rate of precipitation increase.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-184) Sea level rise since 1990 was underestimated in older models, but more recent models agree well with observations.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-185) The 2017 United States-published [National Climate Assessment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Climate_Assessment "National Climate Assessment") notes that "climate models may still be underestimating or missing relevant feedback processes".[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-186) Additionally, climate models may be unable to adequately predict short-term regional climatic shifts.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-187)

A [subset of climate models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_assessment_modelling "Integrated assessment modelling") add societal factors to a physical climate model. These models simulate how population, economic growth, and energy use affect—and interact with—the physical climate. With this information, these models can produce scenarios of future greenhouse gas emissions. This is then used as input for physical climate models and carbon cycle models to predict how atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases might change.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-188)[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-189) Depending on the [socioeconomic scenario](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shared_Socioeconomic_Pathways "Shared Socioeconomic Pathways") and the mitigation scenario, models produce atmospheric CO 2 concentrations that range widely between 380 and 1400 ppm.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-190)

Impacts
-------

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/202505_Escalation_of_extreme_heat_-_247_countries_-_attribution_to_climate_change.svg/330px-202505_Escalation_of_extreme_heat_-_247_countries_-_attribution_to_climate_change.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:202505_Escalation_of_extreme_heat_-_247_countries_-_attribution_to_climate_change.svg)

In virtually all countries and territories around the world, scientists in the field of [extreme event attribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_event_attribution "Extreme event attribution") have concluded that human-caused global warming has increased the number of days of extreme heat events over long-term norms.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-WWA_20250530-191)

### Environmental effects

The environmental effects of climate change are broad and far-reaching, [affecting oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_oceans "Effects of climate change on oceans"), ice, and weather. Changes may occur gradually or rapidly. Evidence for these effects comes from studying climate change in the past, from modelling, and from modern observations.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-192) Since the 1950s, droughts and heat waves have appeared simultaneously with increasing frequency.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-193) Extremely wet or dry events within the [monsoon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monsoon "Monsoon") period have increased in India and East Asia.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-194) Monsoonal precipitation over the Northern Hemisphere has increased since 1980.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-195) The rainfall rate and intensity of [hurricanes and typhoons is likely increasing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_cyclones_and_climate_change "Tropical cyclones and climate change"),[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-USGCRP-2017-196) and the geographic range likely expanding poleward in response to climate warming.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-197) The frequency of tropical cyclones has not increased as a result of climate change.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-198)

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Sea_level_history_and_projections.svg/500px-Sea_level_history_and_projections.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sea_level_history_and_projections.svg)

Historical sea level reconstruction and projections up to 2100 published in 2017 by the U.S. Global Change Research Program[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-199)

Global sea level is rising as a consequence of [thermal expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_expansion "Thermal expansion") and [the melting of glaciers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850 "Retreat of glaciers since 1850") and [ice sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheets "Ice sheets"). Sea level rise has increased over time, reaching 4.8 cm per decade between 2014 and 2023.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-200) Over the 21st century, the IPCC projects 32–62 cm of sea level rise under a low emission scenario, 44–76 cm under an intermediate one and 65–101 cm under a very high emission scenario.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-201)[Marine ice sheet instability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_ice_sheet_instability "Marine ice sheet instability") processes in Antarctica may add substantially to these values,[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-202) including the possibility of a 2-meter sea level rise by 2100 under high emissions.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBamberOppenheimerKoppAspinall2019-203)

Climate change has led to decades of [shrinking and thinning of the Arctic sea ice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline "Arctic sea ice decline").[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-204) While ice-free summers are expected to be rare at 1.5°C degrees of warming, they are set to occur once every three to ten years at a warming level of 2°C.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-205) Higher atmospheric CO 2 concentrations cause more CO 2 to dissolve in the oceans, which is [making them more acidic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_acidification "Ocean acidification").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-206) Because oxygen is less soluble in warmer water,[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-207) its concentrations in the ocean [are decreasing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_deoxygenation "Ocean deoxygenation"), and [dead zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology) "Dead zone (ecology)") are expanding.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-208)

### Tipping points and long-term impacts

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Tipping_points_2022_list.jpeg/500px-Tipping_points_2022_list.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tipping_points_2022_list.jpeg)

Different levels of global warming may cause different parts of Earth's climate system to reach tipping points that cause transitions to different states.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-209)[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-ArmstrongMcKay2022-210)

Greater degrees of global warming increase the risk of passing through '[tipping points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tipping_points_in_the_climate_system "Tipping points in the climate system")'—thresholds beyond which certain major impacts can no longer be avoided even if temperatures return to their previous state.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-211)[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-212) For instance, the [Greenland ice sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet "Greenland ice sheet") is already melting, but if global warming reaches levels between 1.7°C and 2.3°C, its melting will continue until it fully disappears. If the warming is later reduced to 1.5°C or less, it will still lose a lot more ice than if the warming was never allowed to reach the threshold in the first place.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Bochow2023-213) While the ice sheets would melt over millennia, other tipping points would occur faster and give societies less time to respond. The collapse of major [ocean currents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_current "Ocean current") like the [Atlantic meridional overturning circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_meridional_overturning_circulation "Atlantic meridional overturning circulation") (AMOC), and irreversible damage to key ecosystems like the [Amazon rainforest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_rainforest "Amazon rainforest") and [coral reefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral_reefs "Coral reefs") can unfold in a matter of decades.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-ArmstrongMcKay2022-210) The [collapse of the AMOC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrupt_climate_change "Abrupt climate change") would be a severe climate catastrophe, resulting in a cooling of the Northern Hemisphere.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-214)

The long-term [effects of climate change on oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_oceans "Effects of climate change on oceans") include further ice melt, [ocean warming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_temperature#Increasing_temperature_due_to_climate_change "Ocean temperature"), sea level rise, ocean acidification and ocean deoxygenation.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-215) The timescale of long-term impacts are centuries to millennia due to CO 2's long atmospheric lifetime.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-216) The result is an estimated total sea level rise of 2.3 metres per degree Celsius (4.2 ft/°F) after 2000 years.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-217) Oceanic CO 2 uptake is slow enough that ocean acidification will also continue for hundreds to thousands of years.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR5_WG1_Ch1220131112-218) Deep oceans (below 2,000 metres (6,600 ft)) are also already committed to losing over 10% of their dissolved oxygen by the warming which occurred to date.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-219) Further, the [West Antarctic ice sheet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Antarctic_ice_sheet "West Antarctic ice sheet") appears committed to practically irreversible melting, which would increase the sea levels by at least 3.3 m (10 ft 10 in) over approximately 2000 years.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-ArmstrongMcKay2022-210)[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Lau2023-220)[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-Naughten2023-221)

### Nature and wildlife

Recent warming has driven many terrestrial and freshwater species poleward and towards higher [altitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altitudes "Altitudes").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-222) For instance, the range of hundreds of North American birds has shifted northward at an average rate of 1.5 km/year over the past 55 years.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-223) Higher atmospheric CO 2 levels and an extended growing season have resulted in global greening. However, heatwaves and drought have reduced [ecosystem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem "Ecosystem") productivity in some regions. The future balance of these opposing effects is unclear.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_SRCCL_Ch22019133-224) A related phenomenon driven by climate change is [woody plant encroachment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woody_plant_encroachment "Woody plant encroachment"), affecting up to 500 million hectares globally.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-225) Climate change has contributed to the expansion of drier climate zones, such as the [expansion of deserts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desertification "Desertification") in the [subtropics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subtropics "Subtropics").[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-226) The size and speed of global warming is making [abrupt changes in ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecological_threshold "Ecological threshold") more likely.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurnerCalderCummingHughes20201-227) Overall, it is expected that climate change will result in the [extinction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction "Extinction") of many species.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban2015-228)

The oceans have heated more slowly than the land, but plants and animals in the ocean have migrated towards the colder poles faster than species on land.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-229) Just as on land, [heat waves in the ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_heatwave "Marine heatwave") occur more frequently due to climate change, harming a wide range of organisms such as corals, [kelp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelp "Kelp"), and [seabirds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seabirds "Seabirds").[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-230) Ocean acidification makes it harder for [marine calcifying organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_biogenic_calcification "Marine biogenic calcification") such as [mussels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mussel "Mussel"), [barnacles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barnacle "Barnacle") and corals to [produce shells and skeletons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomineralization "Biomineralization"); and heatwaves have [bleached coral reefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral_bleaching "Coral bleaching").[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_SROCC_Summary_for_Policymakers201913-231)[Harmful algal blooms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmful_algal_blooms "Harmful algal blooms") enhanced by climate change and [eutrophication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eutrophication "Eutrophication") lower oxygen levels, disrupt [food webs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_web "Food web") and cause great loss of marine life.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-232) Coastal ecosystems are under particular stress. Almost half of global wetlands have disappeared due to climate change and other human impacts.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_SROCC_Ch52019451-233) Plants have come under increased stress from damage by insects.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change#cite_note-234)
